B005LW5J9O|5.0|In 1417 Poggio Bracciolini, a papal official, found an old manuscript of a work by a Roman poet and philosopher named Lucretius in a remote German monastery.  The manuscript had probably been lying around for hundreds of years, and itself was probably several copies removed from the original.  Lucretius lived in the first century BC.  His work was influenced by earlier philosophers, particularly the Greek Epicurus, who lived in the third century BC.  The work, entitled \\"On the Nature of Things\\", had been lost until Poggio rediscovered it.  Poggio made of copy of it and it was then re-copied and then printed and eventually disseminated throughout Europe.    Lucretius' ideas are astonishingly modern.  Here is a summary of what Lucretius thought (I have borrowed Greenblatt's phrases): matter is composed of invisible particles, and these \\"atoms\\" endlessly recombine in random ways; the universe has no creator or designer, there is no end or purpose to existence, nature ceaselessly experiments, creatures whose combinations of organs enables them to adapt will succeed in establishing themselves until changing circumstances make it impossible for them to survive, the universe was not created for or about humans, there is no reason to believe that human beings as a species will last forever, there are no souls which survive after death, and no afterlife, all religions are superstitious delusions, there are no angels, demons or ghosts, and the highest goal of human life is the pursuit of happiness (thus the famous phrase in our Declaration of Independence).  But this pursuit of happiness is not hedonism, the greatest obstacle to pleasure is not pain, but delusion about what leads to happiness.  The poem is an attempt to show how to lead a good life in the face of an indifferent universe.  The Latin verse is apparently very beautiful.   These ideas were dangerous when Poggio discovered the manuscript and continued to be so all the way through to the Enlightenment.  But Greenblatt shows how Lucretius, once reintroduced into European thought, changed it forever, by influencing European humanists.  The chain of influence leads through Montaigne, touches Shakespeare, and ultimately permeates the Enlightenment.  Jefferson owned five copies of Lucretius in Latin and others in other languages.   One of Lucretius' ideas was that sometimes in the random recombination of atoms there is a \\"swerve\\", a chance variation which leads to unexpected results.  Hence the title of Greenblatt's book.   Greenblatt writes lucidly and is endlessly entertaining.  My favorite part was the description of how books were copied before the invention of the printing press.  As many as fifty monks would sit for hours in a well-lighted room, each at his desk, copying texts.  They were not allowed to talk, and were discourged from trying to understand the texts.  I especially enjoyed reading about this on my Kindle.
B005MYEQ4U|5.0|Woody Allen's 2011 endeavor, Midnight in Paris begins similarly to what you would expect of a Woody Allen picture. Shots of Paris in the morning, quirky music, shots of Paris in the afternoon, music continues, shots of Paris in the evening... I think you can assume the rest. However with Paris in the spotlight instead of Allen's beloved New York, you might expect an obvious loss of affection or romanticism, however there is none at all which is first and foremost a fantastic breath of fresh air.<br /><br />Our hero is Gil, that prototype Woody Allen, self-effacing and neurotic character we've come to know, lovingly played by a surprisingly well-suited Owen Wilson. As the story opens, we find Gil and his fiance Inez (Rachel McAdams) in Paris, along with his less than enthusiastic future in-laws quite openly butting heads in the city he adores and she loathes. Gil is a Hollywood writer who longs to write something more meaningful and has begun on a novel about a man working in a nostalgia shop, selling relics of a more beautiful time past that Gil has yet to find the nerve to let another soul read.<br /><br />Walking back from a wine tasting alone, it is than that magic happens to Gil when the clock strikes midnight and on the windy Parisian street ahead of him appears a gorgeous old car, that opens it's door for him and begins a trip to Paris in the 1920's that has Gil rubbing shoulders with the periods most beloved artists.<br /><br />It is easy to say that this film is formulaic, however I beg to differ. I found this film to be without a doubt my favorite of this year, and possibly my new favorite Woody Allen film. (And I mean that Annie Hall!)<br /><br />Owen Wilson is magnetic and enjoyable in a way I personally have never thought him to be, supported by a brilliant cast including the gorgeous Marion Cotillard, always brilliant Michael Sheen and Corey Stoll as Ernest Hemingway in a performance that absolutely elevated the entire film.<br /><br />It might not be everyone's cup of tea, but Midnight in Paris does something few other films have done by soloing out the true nature of everyone's desire to yearn to live in another time, to fantasize that we'd be happier and more well-suited there instead of where we happen to be. To see such a subject tackled without a shred of existential angst, instead that glimmer of understanding the need for escapism when we're afraid to face our own reality is nothing short of beautiful.something few other films have done by soloing out the true nature of everyone's desire to yearn to live in another time, to fantasize that we'd be happier and more well-suited there instead of where we happen to be. My friend Nate loved classical music and always carried around with him a little portable radio so he could listen to the local classical station. I'd chide Nate a bit and tell him he ought to listen to pop music more and he'd laugh about it. I always used the Beatles as an example of popular music, but he'd never heard them or heard of them (almost hard to believe at the time). One night we were in a tavern enjoying a pitcher of beer and I kept playing Beatles' music for him on the juke box. Much to my amazement, he liked it. Well, A Hard Day's Night had just started a first-run playing at the local theaters and I convinced Nate we ought to go see it. So there were Nate and I sitting in a theater packed with screaming teenagers (we were late 20s) and watching A Hard Day's Night.<br /><br />The movie was highly spoken of by a great majority of movie critics, but everything the Beatles did or said was highly praised in those days. Personally, I didn't enjoy the movie all that much, although it was fun to see the reactions of those days and to see the Beatles perform their songs. The reaction in  the theater we were in could only be described as ecstatic enthusiasm. Actually, the movie was hard to understand because of all the screaming.<br /><br />Well, it's been a long time but I've remembered it well and not too long ago I commemorated the event by buying the \\"A Hard Day's Night\\" CD. I watched it and the accompanying \\"extra\\" materials over the last two nights. My reaction to the movie was just about the same as with my original viewing. It was interesting to see the events of those long ago days as they were documented. Surely, the Beatles reaction was a form of mass hysteria. Just amazing! It was also good to see the Beatles performing in their youth, and I've always loved their music, without question. The movie itself was a hodge-podge of home movies, TV spots, photographs, acted scenes, and what not all. It seemed to have little organization. The sound on my stereo system was poor, at best. The video quality was generally poor. The acting would be bad for a B movie. The joking was stale and unfunny. The \\"clean old man\\" joke grew evermore wearysome. A lot of the Beatles performances looked as if they were lip synced. I fail to see how the critics rated it so highly, perhaps this to was also a form of mass hysteria. In any case, I enjoyed re-watching it, but it seemed long and drawn out and I was glad when it was finally over. Evidently, the entire movie was produced in a couple month's time and. quite frankly, that's what it looks like.<br /><br />A second disc was included and carried commentary from those who were associated with the movie in one way or another. If you're really, really a Beatles fan, then, of course, you must have it. If you're buying it to watch a good movie, well, I think you're going to be disappointed. Perhaps not. In the second and third books, she's just a puppet who rarely thinks for herself. For a protagonist in a dystopian novel about war, Katniss seriously lacks leadership skills and fighting spirit. Her grief, confusion and devastation are understandable but she lacks any personality growth that can make her her own person. Though she sees great tragedy around her, her acquiescent personality lacks the capability to do something about it or at least attempt to make the best of a situation. She's thrust into the shoes of a rebel, but she doesn't have an ounce of rebellious spirit in her in books two and three. It reminded me of this HP on the \\"difference between being dragged into the arena to face a battle to the death and walking into the arena with your head held high....that there was all the difference in the world.\\" Katniss is definitely being dragged into arena. There's anger and hatred after years of oppression, and you can't expect people to easily forgive their oppressors. B002ZG98FU|1.0|I can safely say that this is one of the worst movies I have seen for a long time. It was boring, unintelligent, drawn out, predictable and the actors had next to zero chemistry between them. Rachel McAdams is super cute of course but painfully perky and other than being super cute and perky, she doesnt seem to have much else to offer. The movie itself just doesnt get going either and remains flat throughout. I actually had to finish this in two sittings because it was soooo boring.   There are many lows in this movie but the worst part is the total embarrassment Harrison Ford and Diane Keaton have become. I mean these are two of Hollywood's finest in terms of acting skill and knowing their craft; yet their roles have been reduced to that of bickering, nagging harpies. Especially Harrison Ford, who's always been this sharp, classy and together man, appeared frail, tired, and his hands were trembling. He looked like he really didn't want to do this and as a result delivered an unbelievable performance. To see this legendary actor be reduced to playing a one dimensional, tired and full of stereotypes sidekick to Rachel McAdams and Patrick Wilson, because they are younger and he is older, was very sad. It was a role unworthy of Harrison Ford.   Anyway, said Ford's character is a walking clichee. He plays the typical untouchable, revered star (veteran news anchor) whose track record of issues with historic significance covered puts every newscaster and journalist in CNN to shame. He is a man with a stern look and principles. Principles he likes ot shove into everyone's face. He has a disdain for the materialism and consumerism of our times and wants no part in it. In fact, he looks down on everyone, including the network that hired him and stuffed his pocket with lots of money, as having sold out the art of journalism for sponsorship and ratings. Of course he doesnt have a problem lowering those sacred standards and work for daytime tv himself. A fact conveniently glossed over by the writers. Oh well. At some point he felt like one of those guys who want to uphold principles for the sake of upholding them.   I dont know what's happened to Harrison Ford, if it is the script or him or what, but he did a lousy job if you ask me. He had already done a pretty bad job doing the last installment of Indiana Jones too. Anyway, here he plays a man who's covered significant historic events and who is supposed to be a legend in his field and passionate about his work. This passion is supposed to create tension and be a real challenge for Rachel McAdams' character who not only has a lot to learn from this man she so admires but who, as a producer, also has to worry about ratings and money - the two things Ford hates; yet, instead of working from that angle and thoughtfully laying out how Ford and Adams work that out, all we get is a fizzled out, flat performance portraying a silly, old man reminiscing about the past and holding on to that \\"sixteen-millimeter shrine\\" of his glory days. Ford was not believable as a deep intellectual who lost his bearings (which is what they like us to think). He was dispassionate and boring and just seemed grumpy and tired. His heart wasnt in this performance and that shone through. At some point he was so excited playing this role I thought he would break down and yawn. And let's not even talk about the truly monotonous manner with which he delivers the news. There is no way he can pull off a news anchor with that slow stumble. It just sounded too rehearsed and fake and somehow the producers thought we wouldnt catch it since it is Harrison Ford and who'd criticize Ford.   The movie went downhill from the moment Ford entered and he and Diane Keaton barked at each other like grumpy old people that have fallen victim to juvenile behavior do and extended into comatose lengths.   The love story between McAdams and Patrick Wilson was laughable and totally unbelievable. Patrick Wilson appears to just have been a stand-in, a nameless, faceless hunk to fit the plot element as the handsome dream-man she clearly deserves and who, as a result, doesnt need to be given much of a character other than looking good and being there to move the story along. The specifics of how and why they fall in love are not explored; they have a couple of exchanges and then he becomes the love of her life. Consequently when she says \\"you are the first person I thought about wanting to share this with\\" or something, it looks ridiculous and insincere, because how could she love him, she hasnt exchanged more than 3 sentences with him. After she says that, there is a cut (i.e. we dont get ot see his reaction and what he says) and the next frame opens right in the middle of a conversation between them after she \\"told him\\", where he asks her about her JOB. So to recap, this is how the scene went down (not in so many words):  She: \\"I love you, you are my soul mate. You are the first person I had to tell about this\\"   He: \\"Uh-huh. That's great. So, tell me about your JOB and promotion\\".   Suffice it so say, the love story was totally superfluous in addition to really not adding anything to the movie other than being an all too familiar plot device. In order for the main character to be successful in her career, it is imperative that the obligatory love interest be there too else the formula is incomplete.   One of the issues with this movie was that it wasnt clear what this was trying to be. A love story? No, because that wasnt worked out. Was it supposed to be about TV Shows and the hard world of TV production? No, because that wasnt worked out either and the TV setting just seemed to be the incidental backdrop. Was it supposed to be about disillusionment in journalism? No, because - as mentioned above - Ford just did not deliver anything worthwhile and thought provoking in that regard but clichees and banalities. So it is really unclear what they wanted this to be. Drama, rom-com, comedy, comedy-drama, romance, journalism, hard work, working with legends whose glory days are behind them? To me, it seemed a bit like a neurotic mess.   So all in all this was a boring, plain movie with flat characters and unintelligent dialogues. It was also very unworthy of the actors. Of course after writing this review I found that JJ Abrams produced it so it doesnt surprise me at all that this has turned out to be such a vacuous, empty and boring star-studded piece of garbage.

0060873086|3.0|Read by John Bedford Lloyd<br />Duration: 14 hours<br /><br />Michael Crichton died in 2008 and left 'Micro' as an unfinished manuscript. I have no idea how much of this book is actually Crichton's and how much belongs to Richard Preston. To me it felt like a typical Crichton novel.<br /><br />A typical Crichton novel for me is a mixed bag. It has grand themes - truly big, big ideas with foundations in real science. Grand themes about the dangers of too much innovation without enough ethical considerations, lots of Gee Whiz stuff (think of the movie 'Jurassic Park' where the paleontologists are mesmerized when they first see the dinosaurs) and laughable plot lines with sketch characters (the worst for me was 'State of Fear' in which the big menacing bad guys were wedging themselves into Toyota Priuses as they stalked their opponents - yes, the Prius, the ultimate pursuit car!).<br /><br />This book has all of that in spades.<br /><br />The plot revolves around NanoGen, a Hawaiian start up company that has figured out (or stolen) how to shrink full-sized objects down to a very, very small size. People are about one half of an inch tall when they are shrunk. NanoGen claims to be using the technology to thoroughly search the Hawaiian rain forest for biological discoveries that  could be used to help create medicines. Their plan is to search the micro-world bit by bit (literally square foot by square foot) in tiny detail so that even creatures that cannot be seen with the naked eye can be harvested and investigated for possible uses in a bio-technology laboratory.<br /><br />But, the bad guy in charge of the project also wants to use these tiny robots as weapons and has made plans to corrupt the original vision of NanoGen's founders. So, murder and mayhem result and soon enough we are following a group of college graduate students who have been shrunken and dumped into the rain forest in an attempt to get rid of even more witnesses (why weren't they immediately squished and flushed down the toilet? The bad guy is so over-the-top in his sadism that he wants to prolong their punishment, which of course eventually backfires.).<br /><br />So, when our seven college graduate students are dumped in the rain forest (in the micro world as Crichton/Preston usually refer to it) we have several scenes that are reminiscent of Jurassic Park, except we don't have T. Rex and Velociraptors. Rather, we have centipedes, spiders, wasps and ants - all armored and all very dangerous to very tiny people. This part of the book is by far the best - the descriptions of the bugs, their habits, their defenses and their weapons are all fascinating. If it weren't for these details I would have to rate this novel poorly, but the descriptions are entertaining in and of themselves.<br /><br />John Bedford Lloyd's narration of the book was solid but really did little to enhance or detract from the book.  His voices were solid. To be fair, most of the plot was inane, so it was not like he was working with a literary classic. His deep voice did add a lot to the menacing descriptions of the bugs.he-top in his sadism that he wants to prolong their punishment, which of course eventually backfires.). B0059XTUB8|3.0|It has a certain surface charm to it, particularly if you're interested in old movies and movie stars. She's not embarrassing or inept, just unaffecting. She merely rustles up images of the real thing, who, we're reminded, was actually dull. Her inner circle may have sucked up to her like she was a brilliantly innovative and intellectual devotee to the craft of acting, but the bottom line is that people were fascinated with Monroe because they wanted to have sex with her or because they wanted to be like her, and that was why she was famous and adored (and hated, too, I imagine). The film offers up the classic \\"I'm-beautiful-so-no-one-thinks-I-can-act\\" portrait of Marilyn but declines to comment on it. This movie tries to make an awards grab without any fingers... 1594630844|5.0|30 Lessons for Living is a book to be savored.  Written by Karl Pillemer, gerontologist and researcher at Cornell University, it is a product of the author's Legacy Project, a series of surveys and interviews conducted of those over 65, the \\"experts\\" on living to whom the book is dedicated.  The purpose of the research is to identify the keys to the good life that those of a certain age have managed to uncover.    The book is broken down into several major themes having to do with such things as marriage, parenting, careers, health, and most generally how to achieve happiness.  The lessons presented, with some exceptions--it's normal to have a favorite child--are not exactly earth-shattering.  But the beauty of the book comes from the diversity of voices and individual expression that refract and enrich the observations from people who are remarkably aware and self-reflective.  Unfortunately, for stylistic reasons and because--at their age they know better--the thoughts and advice given here will be lost on youth.  But for those who, like this writer, are on the cusp of seniorhood, the insights on lives well-lived are both instructive and heartening.  This is a very good book for an individual reader contemplating life's \\"golden\\" years, even better if it can be shared across generations.

030788743X|2.0|UPDATE: 6/5/2012 -<br /><br />Goodreads has a HUGE banner ad for this book and the new cover for the paperback edition, which prompted me to update my review.<br /><br />This is a classic do-not-judge-a-book-by-its-cover warning: If you think the cover, and the world it portrays, looks interesting, please be aware that the cover represents about 5% of the book. This 5% is the most (and really, the only) interesting aspect of the book: the author's view of the future real world. It's a well-done dystopia inspired by the original Donkey Kong, Hard Hat Mack, and other 80s video games. The other 95% of the book is set in a boring, predictable, digital world, and unless you're wearing neon leg warmers, a side ponytail, and carrying a boom box RIGHT NOW, there is nothing of interest in much of this story.<br /><br />---<br /><br />I don't know why I finished this book. The plot was fun, I guess, but entirely predictable.<br /><br />The plot of the book is this: a crazy old man who invented this insanely realistic (and therefore completely unbelievable) MMO video game loved the 80s. He wants everyone to share his infatuation with music, movies, TV shows and video games of the 80s, so he creates a contest before dies. In order to win his multi-billion-dollar fortune and control of OASIS (the aforementioned MMO), people have to study all sorts of not-so-obscure 80s pop culture references. The real world is kind of interesting and post-apocalyptic, but under-utilized.<br /><br />OASIS is too detailed to be believable. Yes, this is sci-fi, but it takes place in this world, a world in which the 80s happened. It name drops many MMOs, suspiciously neglecting the thing OASIS most resembles: Second Life. A physics engine as comprehensive and detailed as OASIS's is unbelievable. So is the way all the players play the game as though it's a real world. Most people don't play games as though they're real life.<br /><br />The whole point of the book seems to be that the author really liked the 80s. You should too! If you don't get any of the references, don't worry: he'll explain every one of them in intricate detail, taking the fun out of the \\"Oh, I get that!\\" point of pop culture references. And... that's it. The novel never says much of substance. I wanted more. In the end, the only statement the novel makes is that reality is better than virtual reality, pretty much negating everything you've just read.<br /><br />I was born in 1983, so I don't possess the 80s nostalgia that so many people have. The retro factor only works if you were there in the first place. I played a lot of video games, but if I never have to watch another John Cusack movie, it'll be too soon. I would like to give it an extra star for pop culture references, but I personally didn't care for any of them. Plus, the author does nothing to convince you the 80s were cool if you don't already think so.<br /><br />The only reason this book isn't marketing toward young adults is because today's young adults weren't born in the 80s and unlike the hero of this book, Wade Watts, they couldn't care less about the 80s. Smart kids. As I said, the author does nothing to convince you otherwise.ked the 80s. You should too! 0307454541|3.0|I read this two years ago but never reviewed it. I work in a bookstore and have had lots of customers ask me about the book now that the David Fincher movie is coming out. \\"Would I like it?\\" they ask, as though I know their reading preferences. My stock answer: \\"It depends on how you feel about serial killers and magazine publishing.\\" The Girl with the Dragon Tattoo is about a 70-20 mix of the two, with literary sensation Lisbeth Salander on the side.<br /><br />Old women love this book. I know this because back when the Swedish movie came out, I went to see it and it was me and the Red Hat Society in the theater. If I hadn't sold the book to multiple AARP members, I would have thought I was in the wrong screening room.<br /><br />Perhaps the senior citizens are having violent revenge fantasies about men who done them wrong. That's a big part of the book, and the most compelling to read. Lisbeth Salander is independent and diabolical. There's also intriguing family drama in the frozen Swedish countryside. Oh, and a few hundred pages about magazine publishing, finances, and blackmail. Feel free to skip those, although the polyamorous editor, Erika Berger, is an interesting side character.<br /><br />Unfortunately, the book gets bogged down in details. No one ever opens a laptop without giving you a full run-down of its specs, down to processing speed and amount of RAM. The rest of the writing is dry and basic. As with all translated books, it's hard to tell if the translator left out the spice or if the original writing was a dull concoction to begin with.<br /><br />Dragon Tattoo is a stand-alone book. Yes, there are two more, but they merely function as a sequel, not a must-read trilogy. Plus, they succeed in demolishing any and all of Lisbeth Salander's independence and credibility, turning her from vigilante to a lonely man's wet dream. That's something else that drags down this series: Larsson's Gary Stu-ing of the whole thing. Mikael Blomqust, homely coffee-chugging journalist beds practically every woman he stumbles across. I think \\"Ghost Protocol\\" incites fear, loathing, and ridicule vis-a-vis the \\"Orient\\" in a jokey kind of manner.<br /><br />\\"Orient\\" is not a geographical term, but a cultural one. It is a gap that separated \\"the West\\" form \\"the Rest\\". The hallmarks are: a reflexive cultural contempt and even mild racism, expressed in patronizing superiority vis-&agrave;-vis other cultures. It has become, with few exceptions, a common thing in the Hollywood today.  For me, as a Russian-American, it looks like Russia has been placed firmly by the Hollywood in the realm of cultural \\"Orient\\". 030726999X|5.0|After being shot in the head and having various wounds on her body, Salander, our odd but brilliant heroine from the previous books,  is placed in isolation in a hospital. Slowly recovering from her ordeal, she develops a positive relationship with the attending physician who secures a cell phone with a Palm browser. Accessing a hidden server connection and as she is awaiting trial, Salander is able to hack into local law enforcement computers, attorney computers, and communicate with Mikael Blomkvist. In spite of her injuries, she is once again at her intellectual best with remarkable computer skills and a photographic memory. With her evil, terrorist father two rooms away, Salander stays alert, aware that he wants to finish the murder her tried earlier. Her defense attorney, Blomkvist's very able sister, convinces Salander to write an autobiographical account of the social abuses, the rape from her guardian, and the events that led to the bullet in her head.<br /><br />Blomkvist and staff at the Millenium begin working to uncover a secret organization within the state security force. Finding a plot that includes a massive coverup of a defector and the abuses and terror he launched on Sweden many years before, Blomkvist writes a book to blow the top off the secret organization. Sadly, owner and editor Erika Berger leaves the Millenium for a better position with a larger newspaper. Almost immediately she encounters harrassment and threats from an unknown source followed by an awareness of corruption at a higher level. Knowing she has walked into a serious conflict of interest, she is torn over her loyalties to her former paper and her current employers. Resolving the problem aggressively, Erika becomes the victim of defamation and a whitewashing of the corruption.<br /><br />The organization that has operated for years apart from any kind of oversight and making its own rules slowly gets unraveled by the amazing computer skills of Salander and the tenacity of Blomkvist. Salander's group of defenders that includes her physician, her former guardian, Milton security, and the employees at Millenium join together to help Salander's attorney reveal the truth. The trial is a stunning reversal for Salander and we finally see justice served to those who desired to see Salander's life destroyed.<br /><br />With non-stop cerebral and physical action, The Girl Who Kicked the Hornets Nest is a fantastic conclusion to the series. Lots of coffee, violence, sex, confusion, corruption, fear, brilliance, medicine, and social abuses accompany this high octane legal and political thriller. Salander herself is a new hero for the modern age with her genius and courage finding fruition in computer investigations and a creative approach to life. In a kind of social tug of war with obvious antagonists and protagonists, readers find plenty of people to love and to hate, and a wide variety of emotions between. Cheering for her success but despising the causes of her predicaments, we share in her suspicions and empathize with her distrust of authority. Her own desire for indepence and isolation is admirable but impossible as she slowly comes to terms with the need to trust certain people by providing a clear and candid account of her experiences.<br /><br />The women are strong in this novel and Mikael Blomkvist is a little too good to be true at times...does he have any flaws? The book suffers at times from excessive descriptions and interruptions that do not seem to develop into integrated events. The author, now deceased, obviously had a great story to tell and the skill to present it, but occasionally got bogged down in demonstrating the stupidity and inanity of particular kinds of male leadership. Sometimes we are set up for a situation that never really happens, such as Monica's physical prowess never was used for anything in particular, almost like a runway built that is never used. It makes the story feel a little uneven at times with jagged edges that are unresolved.<br /><br />Those things aside, there are many lessons within these pages, but they are masked by an exciting plot that is turbo-charged to hold the reader's attention. Well-written and containing many sideline stories of political war, terror, corruption, and social misgivings, this story along with the other two books are page-turning adventures for modern times. Readers need to know that some rough scenes, language, and sex help paint a realistic picture of a young lady who was shaped by her DNA and environment. But rather than becoming a social commentary on how power can corrupt leaders and how often we turn a blind eye to the truth or how often we assume and inflict judgment on those who are different, we find a walloping good story rich in description and muscular in emotion. This book is highly recommended and reminds us of the kinds of abuses to which many children and teens are subjected. It also reminds us that there are times we must learn to trust the ones who do not mean harm. The filmmakers set themselves boundaries as regarded what a moon mission would be like in the early to mid-70s, and never once nudged their way past to score a cheap scare or thrill.  That kind of integrity shouldn't be overlooked, and it made for a few chilling scenes that would not have been possible if they had succumbed to the temptation to, say, introduce some new, ultra-secret technology we've never heard of, or given the actors more room to breathe, pun intended.  For examples that either aren't spoilers or were in the official trailer:  The sense of menace accomplished through context and posture of a space-suited American astronaut standing there and all you're hearing is his radio-carried voice; a climactic scare that comes to us directly from the laws of physics; a constant sense of claustrophobia when inside the spacecraft (particularly the orbiting Command Module); the hair-standing-on-end statement \\"the flag is gone.\\"    If you're an Apollo nut, like I am, there are as I mentioned several things that are simply inconsistent with history or science, and you can easily choose to be aggravated by them, but honestly, they're easy to ignore in the story and setting and atmosphere (pun not intended).  There are a few just as troubling things about the alien monsters.  Again, to the extent you give the film demerits for these, please do grade it on a curve considering just about any other horror or science fiction movie, and I think you'll find it does ok.  I don't get the sense you're meant to leave this film convinced of the possibility of a worldwide government coverup of a nearby alien menace, and if you don't consider it a failure that it doesn't so convince you, you're apt to be entertained in the way quick little 1:20 scary stories are supposed to entertain.  It makes you chuckle, jump, try and will the characters to do and not do things, smile and wince, and darned if it doesn't feel more authentically like the moon than almost any other picture set there.

As it turned out, ALW wasn't totally happy with the way the show \\"worked\\" on stage. When plans were underway for the second production, the Australian director (who has an international reputation, and is highly respected by ALW), told ALW exactly what was wrong with the production, and ALW completely agreed. He gave the show over to the Australian production team to do with it as they will - he had nothing to do with the revisions until he saw it in final rehearsal. He was totally elated, and the Australian production is the one that will go on Broadway. He is considering closing the London production, and re-opening with this new version, which is absolutely stunning. Act one is among the most spectacular things I have seen on a stage, and the music, of course, id wonderful, superbly sung and acted. The DVD/Blu-ray, coming out mid 2012, is of the Melbourne production. Musically, there have not been many changes, although the opening is quite different. The more I hear of ALW's music, the higher is my respect for his musicianship. The score, like most of his, has melodic songs, as you might find in the earlier forms of musical comedy. But now, in this day of musical drama, many of the songs are dramatic, more operatic, to push the dramatic elements of the story along. At one time, I felt that ALW lacked a style of his own. But I have since realised that he turns his style to match the story. When he has a serious story, like Sunset Boulevard and Woman in White (which failed more because it was above what the average theatre goer wants, rather than any of its weaknesses), he includes more dramatic, rather opera-like material to enhance the drama. And these days, he can afford to have flops, as he is worth more than Stephen Spielberg). It takes a while to get accustomed, especially if you don't like classical music, to some of the songs, but I believe he is one of the greatest composers we have seen for musical theatre, and this may be his best work. I don't think it will succeed as well as the original Phantom for several reason's, but the significant one is that there is a lower content of \\"general audience appeal\\" songs. This show has so much to offer, especially in its reworked form, that it wont be a flop - but I doubt it will be his most commercially successful work, even if it is possibly his musical peak.reatest composers we have seen for musical theatre, and this may be his best work. Young Piscine, \\"Pi\\", Patel details the shortcomings of his own name and his childhood in India with numerous curiosities and triumphs that set his childhood apart from the average kid. His father runs and owns a zoo which becomes a critical piece in Pi's growth and later survival while stranded at sea for over half a year with only a few surviving zoo animals as companions on a small rescue boat.  I found myself continually bemused and emotionally attached to Pi's antics and cleverness in survival which speaks especially to Mr. Martel's ingenuity and creativity in the development of this fantasy adventure. I was, and readers most likely feel the same, continuously considering how well Mr. Martel was able to relay and inspire feelings.   My best example of this comes towards the end of the second portion of the book when Pi has become blind with hunger and suddenly another castaway appears who is French and blind as well. I found myself utterly bewildered attempting to ascertain what was going on. However, this confusion is likely due to Martel's hand and displays the confusion and blindness that Pi himself must have felt being blind and suddenly meeting another person after tens of days with no companion but a Bengal tiger. It was almost kafka-esque.  I would be detracting from my duty and obligation if I did not mention the spiritual undertones and discussion in this novel. Pi is a practicing Hindu, Christian, and Muslim who makes compelling arguments and points about loyalty to one religion and ultimately the larger picture of what belief and piety truly is.  I was, overall, very impressed with \\"Life of Pi\\" and consider it something that should be put on anyone's list to read if your interests lay in adventure, survival, and the new. My only criticism lay in wanting to know the true occurrences and events that surround the plot!  ~Kaptica

Big mistake!     3)  If on the other hand you think this is CD is worth two Grammy nominations, I'm not sure that you and I are in the same universe.         I put this CD on my Christmas list after seeing Plant and the band (which includes Patti Griffin and Buddy Miller) on a PBS concert.  Though the album and the PBS concert share very little material, I like them both equally, but for different reasons.  The concert seemed to be more of a group effort, but Plant is center stage on the CD.        On first play, I was rather lukewarm toward the disk.  It wasn't at all what I expected.  But the next few listens grew increasingly rewarding.  There are many highlights along the way, from the infectious, percussive rhythms  of `Angel Dance' to the haunting and eerie `Monkey' to the Beatlesque `Can't Buy My Love' to the doo-woppish accompaniment of `Falling in Love Again'.  There is a lot to like here.  The only song that doesn't \\"do it for me\\" is the second track, 'House of Cards'.  Though it's not horrible, I think it is by far the weakest cut on the CD.         But, as far as ex-rockers go, this CD is nowhere near as satisfying as Bruce Springsteen's \\"Seeger Sessions\\" set from a few years back.  That set had a more unpolished raunchy sound that came about because a majority of the songs were recorded `live' in Springsteen's place.  That live feel is sorely missing from Plant's CD.  But, by another comparison, Band of Joy is far superior to Rod Stewart's stoic, lifeless visits to the Great American Songbook.         I haven't heard any of these songs previously, so I can't really tell that they're better than or worse than the originals.  Plant sounds like he has a genuine affinity for the material, and seems to be having fun recording it, and the rest of the band also seems to be enjoying the ride - not to mention the listener.  I can tell you that this is definitely worthy of more than a few spins in your player.

The movie is primarily about family and unconditional love for your family. The departure of his older brother and father made Tommy the bitter man he is while it is hard to feel his pain, he shows his warm human side when he talks to a close friend on the phone later in the movie. Brendan is a more straightforward character who lays out his goal out on the floor: win the MMA tournament, get the money, live a happy life; in contrast to Tommy whose goal is unknown until about halfway through the movie. There are key scenes in the film where the viewers can get a full perspective of the relationships between the chracters, and these intimate moments whether it be Brendan and the dad, Tommy and the dad, or Brendan and Tommy, it's those scenes between the family that carries the film to making it the near classic it is.<br /><br />When watching the film you realize that rarely do films nowadays really contain complicated family relationships such as these, sure Warrior has it's cliches, but it really is an uplifting story. The story is told from a horse's point of view and the author does a masterful job in allowing readers to imagine what it might be like to be a horse especially in the 1910s before the brink of World War I.  The reading is easy at times. Morpurgo is very descriptive about life and the surroundings. Even from Joey's point of view, the reader will understand what the horses must go through in a human war. In fact, the novel makes a point that horses are beautiful creatures but who are suffered man's indignity in wars.  The horses' role in wars before cars became readily and easily available was an essential factor in fighting the enemies. I believe the novel will be a must read in middle school and high school reading lists within a year.  Morpurgo writes for a younger audiences but even older audiences can enjoy his writing as well. This book is quite wonderful, sad, and hopeful at the end. There is a short interview with the author in the end of the book. Also, the novel would be more useful if it featured maps and a brief review of World War I to help the reader. B0000DJZA5|5.0|I have read the many reviews here from good to bad. Primarily all points of view have some credibility. There are many reason this release fails and just as many as to justify its release. But i think what needs to be remembered here is the chronological events prior to the official release of the album back in 1970. Originally set for an early 1969 release this album was pushed back on a day to day basis until its eventual release in March 1970. Along the way the Beatles even found necessity (for many reasons) to release an originally unplanned album \\"Abbey Road\\" for a rush Christmas release. Prior to this many \\"entrepreneurs were able to release unofficial release of the \\"Get Back\\"/\\"Let It Be\\" album with varying degrees of completeness and quality. Thus giving birth to the commercial bootleg. Some of these titles sold 10 of thousands of copies often being copied in other regions of distribution straight form someone else's vinyl copy (snaps, crackles, pops, and all). These were essential for any Beatles fan. I still have mine. With the release of Let It Be...Naked we now have the Beatles own statement on Bootlegs and their \\"official\\" version and digital release. Well Done. This is not supposed to be stunning unfinished unrelease traacks form the vault, but merely a documentation of the period just before the official release but now in best ever sound quality and as close to the original direction of the release than even the prior official release. This was supposed to be a bare bones no overdubs one take fits all production. Here it is, enjoy!

If you've missed it somehow, I suggest you buy a copy and watch it now... though I wouldn't necessarily recommend you fork out the extra for the Special Edition.  The Two Disc version is packed with extras but personally I didn't think they added up to much. They've managed to gather together all the important names behind the film - including Minghella and Zaentz - but I thought the interviews were a bit bland and lacking insight. There's definitely not enough from the cast. The long interview with Michael Ondaatje (who wrote the novel upon which the film is based) is rather boring and seems to go on forever. Also included is a made for TV 'Making of' (which is all soundbite and no substance) and a featurette on the real Almasy which is myth busting... but not in a good way (I would have preferred to keep my romantic notions of the Count!). The thing I liked the most was the deleted scenes. I think they made the right decision omitting these from the final cut (esp the Kip/Hannah minefield scene) though all the footage with the ostrich is intriguing!  Overall a 5 star film, 3 star extras. Bit of a wasted opportunity I feel and given that we'll never hear Anthony Minghella talk about the film again, a shame.
